Generated Hypothesis Portfolio

H1 primary score 0.505; inference inferred
In perishable inventory settings with stochastic spoilage, the OR→LLM pipeline from P1 will underperform relative to a specialized OR algorithm that explicitly models spoilage, because LLMs lack the ability to reason about probabilistic spoilage rates and optimal disposal decisions.
H2 incremental score 0.415; inference inferred
The OR→LLM pipeline's advantage over OR alone is largest when demand exhibits regime shifts or seasonality, and smallest under stable demand, because LLMs' contextual reasoning is most valuable when patterns change.
Both selected hypotheses are shown on the front page; treat new ideas as audit targets, not proven facts.

Review Walkthrough

  • 1. Provide input: submit a paper PDF, paper URL, arXiv ID, or research guidance through the CLI or an optional chat wrapper.
  • 2. Run agent: the pipeline records every tool step and stores reusable memory.
  • 3. Open this page: explain the dashboard before drilling into details.
  • 4. Show audit logic: GREEN is direct support, YELLOW is partial/inferred, RED is failure or contradiction.

1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: 5348a15c-1776-4717-81ce-5da262bc1e48

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

AI Agents for Inventory Control:

Authors: Human-LLM-OR Complementarity, Jackie Baek, Yaopeng Fu, Will Ma, Tianyi Peng

Abstract: Inventory control is a fundamental operations problem in which ordering decisions are tradi- tionally guided by theoretically grounded operations research (OR) algorithms. However, such algorithms often rely on rigid modeling assumptions and can perform poorly when demand distributions shift or relevant contextual information is unavailable. Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have generated interest in AI agents that can reason flexibly and in- corporate rich contextual signals, but it remains unclear how best to incorporate LLM-based methods into traditional decision-making pipelines. We study how OR algorithms, LLMs, and humans can interact and complement each other in a multi-period inventory control setting. We construct InventoryBench, a benchmark of over 1,000 inventory instances spanning both synthetic and real-world demand data, designed to stress-test decision rules under demand shifts, seasonality, and uncertain lead times. Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isola- tion, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes. We further investigate the role of humans through a controlled classroom experiment that embeds LLM recommendations into a human-in-the-loop decision pipeline. Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone. Beyond this population-level finding, we formalize an individual-level complementarity effect and derive a distribution-free lower bound on the fraction of individuals who benefit from AI collaboration; empirically, we find this fraction to be substantial. Taken together, our results provide evidence that effective inventory management benefits from a complementary system in which OR provides heuristics and calculators, LLMs contribute world knowledge and contextual reasoning, and human judgment adds value beyond automated decision making.

Key claims:

  • Beyond this population-level finding, we formalize an individual-level complementarity effect and derive a distribution-free lower bound on the fraction of individuals who benefit from AI collaboration; empirically, we find this fraction to be substantial.
  • Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone.
  • However, such algorithms often rely on rigid modeling assumptions and can perform poorly when demand distributions shift or relevant contextual information is unavailable.
  • Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) have generated interest in AI agents that can reason flexibly and incorporate rich contextual signals, but it remains unclear how best to incorporate LLM-based methods into traditional decision-making pipelines.

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Complementarity of OR, LLMs, and Humans in Inventory Control: P1 and P2 demonstrate that OR algorithms, LLM agents, and humans each bring distinct strengths to inventory decisions: OR provides structural discipline and mathematical precision, LLMs contribute contextual reasoning and world knowledge, and humans offer validation and oversight. The OR→LLM pipeline outperforms either alone, and human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either alone, indicating genuine complementarity.
    Evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 8 Evidence 9 Evidence 11
  • T2 Data-Driven Inventory Management Under Uncertainty: P3 (perishable e-groceries) and P6 (S&OP) emphasize data-driven approaches to handle multiple sources of uncertainty (demand, spoilage, lead time). P3 uses probabilistic information and stochastic lookahead policies; P6 uses simulation-optimization with plan stability objectives. Both highlight the value of rich data and predictive analytics, but neither incorporates LLM-based reasoning.
    Evidence: Evidence 23 Evidence 40 Evidence 24
  • T3 Endogenous Demand and Heterogeneous Customers in Inventory Theory: P4 reviews inventory models that relax the assumptions of homogeneous customers and exogenous demand, incorporating customer choice and demand endogeneity. This contrasts with the standard OR models used in P1, which assume exogenous demand. The tension suggests that OR algorithms may underperform when customer heterogeneity and endogenous demand are present.
    Evidence: Evidence 29 Evidence 28 Evidence 32

Gaps

  • G1: The InventoryBench (P1) does not include instances with perishable goods, spoilage, or multi-echelon structures, which are common in real-world supply chains (P3, P7). The complementarity findings may not generalize to these settings. Why it matters: Perishable inventory and multi-echelon systems introduce additional complexities (spoilage, coordination) that could alter the relative strengths of OR, LLMs, and humans.
    Evidence: Evidence 5 Evidence 25
  • G2: No study in the connected papers tests LLM-based inventory agents on problems with endogenous demand or heterogeneous customers (P4). The OR algorithms in P1 assume exogenous demand, so the LLM's ability to reason about customer behavior remains unexplored. Why it matters: If LLMs can infer customer preferences and price sensitivity from text, they could outperform OR algorithms in settings with heterogeneous customers and endogenous demand.
    Evidence: Evidence 2 Evidence 28
  • G3: The human-AI experiment in P1 is a controlled classroom study; its external validity to real-world inventory managers with domain expertise and competing pressures is unknown. Why it matters: Real-world managers may have different trust patterns, cognitive biases, or domain knowledge that affect human-AI complementarity.
    Evidence: Evidence 7 Evidence 8

Tensions

  • X1: P1 claims that OR algorithms rely on rigid assumptions and perform poorly under demand shifts, while P4 argues that inventory theory can incorporate endogenous demand and heterogeneity. This suggests that the OR algorithms used in P1 may be a subset of richer OR models that already address some of the limitations.
    Evidence: Evidence 2 Evidence 28
  • X2: P1 finds that human-AI teams outperform AI alone, contradicting prior findings that human-AI collaboration often degrades performance. This tension suggests that the nature of the task (inventory control) or the design of the AI system (OR-LLM pipeline) may be critical for achieving complementarity.
    Evidence: Evidence 8 Evidence 17

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: The idea of combining structured optimization (OR) with flexible reasoning (LLMs) from inventory control can be transferred to other operational domains such as supply chain network design (P12) or sales and operations planning (P6), where similar trade-offs between mathematical precision and contextual adaptation exist. Rationale: Both domains involve multi-period decision-making under uncertainty with structured models and contextual signals. The OR-LLM complementarity pattern could be applied to S&OP to balance plan stability (OR) with market intelligence (LLM).
    Evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 39
  • C2: The use of probabilistic information to quantify uncertainty in perishable inventory (P3) can inform the design of LLM prompts for inventory agents (P1), enabling LLMs to better reason about spoilage risks and lead-time variability. Rationale: P3 shows how probabilistic information guides data collection investments; LLMs could be prompted with such probabilistic summaries to improve their contextual reasoning about perishable goods.
    Evidence: Evidence 24 Evidence 11

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H1 - In perishable inventory settings with stochastic spoilage, the OR→LLM pipeline from P1 will underperform relative to a specialized OR algorithm that explicitly models spoilage, because LLMs lack the ability to reason about probabilistic spoilage rates and optimal disposal decisions.

Total score: 0.505 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.7 Feasibility: 0.8 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.3

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: P1's benchmark excludes perishable goods (G1). P3 shows that probabilistic information about spoilage is critical for optimal decisions. LLMs may not effectively incorporate such probabilistic constraints without explicit prompting.

Novelty position: Extends complementarity findings to perishable inventory, a common real-world setting not tested in P1.

How to test it: Extend InventoryBench with perishable instances (varying spoilage rates, lead times). Compare OR→LLM, OR-only, LLM-only, and the stochastic lookahead policy from P3 on profit and waste metrics.

Premise evidence: Evidence 6 Evidence 24 Evidence 25

Supporting papers: P1: AI Agents for Inventory Control: P3: Managing inventories for perishable e-groceries: The value of probabilistic information

Risks:

  • LLMs might still add value by detecting spoilage patterns from textual data; OR algorithm may already be near-optimal.
incremental

H2 - The OR→LLM pipeline's advantage over OR alone is largest when demand exhibits regime shifts or seasonality, and smallest under stable demand, because LLMs' contextual reasoning is most valuable when patterns change.

Total score: 0.415 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.6 Feasibility: 0.9 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.4

Why retained: Retained as a more conservative A+B, transfer, or implementation-adjacent direction that is distinct from the primary proposal. It may be useful follow-up work, but it should not be read as the most novel hypothesis.

Motivation: P1 shows OR algorithms perform poorly under demand shifts (2) and LLMs detect regime changes (9). This suggests a boundary condition: LLM value depends on demand variability.

Novelty position: Provides a precise, testable boundary condition for the complementarity result, going beyond average performance.

How to test it: On InventoryBench, compute the performance gap (OR→LLM minus OR-only) for each instance. Regress gap on demand stability metrics (e.g., regime shift frequency, seasonality strength).

Premise evidence: Evidence 2 Evidence 9 Evidence 6

Supporting papers: P1: AI Agents for Inventory Control:

Risks:

  • Effect may be small or confounded with other instance features.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 14; by status: {'needs_human_review': 8, 'evidence_inspired': 6}; by color: {'yellow': 14}; precision evidence per component: None

On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. We design LLM agents for inventory control (Subsection 3.1), construct a benchmark of 1,320 instances to evaluate them (Subsection 4.1), and build a web-based game for human–AI interaction (Subsection 5.1). Evidence 58CL3 supportAI Agents for Inventory Control:pdf:method:p1
CL3 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
We study how OR algorithms, LLMs, and humans can interact and complement each other in a multi-period inventory control setting. We construct InventoryBench, a benchmark of over 1,000 inventory instances spanning both synthetic and real-world demand data, designed to stress-test decision rules under demand shifts, seasonality, and uncertain lead times. Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes.
Evidence 59CL3 supportAI Agents for Inventory Control:pdf:method:p1
CL3 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes. We further investigate the role of humans through a controlled classroom experiment that embeds LLM recommendations into a human-in-the-loop decision pipeline. Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone. Beyond this population-level finding, we formalize an individual-level complementarity effect and derive a distribution-free lower bound on the fraction of individuals who benefit from AI collaboration; empirically, we find this fraction to be substantial. Taken together, our results provide evidence that effective inventory management benefits from a complementary system in which OR provides heuristics and calculators, LLMs contribute world knowledge and contextual reasoning, and human judgment adds value beyond automated decision making.

7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: MEDIUM

Conclusion: Similar prior work was found; the idea may need sharper differentiation.

Reason: 1 near match(es) share method, target problem, or abstract-level framing.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

9. Claim Support Audit

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

GREEN + directVerified reference and explicit evidence directly support this exact claim.
YELLOW + indirect/backgroundEvidence motivates or contextualizes the claim but does not prove it.
RED + unsupported/absentNo usable evidence, invalid reference, contradiction, or clear unsupported claim.
no_specific_error_detectedNo four-category error was detected; this can still be YELLOW if support is indirect.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

OR Algorithm LLM Agent Human Structural discipline: base-stock heuristics, mathematical precision Contextual reasoning: pattern detection, world knowledge Validation & oversight: catches LLM failures, detects anomalies recommendation reasoning / guidance Figure 2: The complementary interaction pattern of OR algorithms, LLM agents, and humans. On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. The finding that humans add value is particularly noteworthy: achieving genuine human–AI complementarity is widely recognized as difficult, with systematic evidence showing that human–AI teams frequently fail to outperform the better of human and AI acting independently (Bansal et al., 2021; Vaccaro et al., 2024; Hemmer et al., 2025). Our results suggest that inventory control—with its sequential decisions, delayed feedback, and rich contextual signals—is a setting where human judgment genuinely complements AI. We design LLM agents for inventory control (Subsection 3.1), construct a benchmark of 1,320 instances to evaluate them (Subsection 4.1), and build a web-based game for human–AI interaction (Subsection 5.1). The OR→LLM pipeline achieves the best overall performance (Subsection 4.2), but disaggregating by lead time (Subsection 4.3), instance family (Subsection 4.4), and critical fractile (Subsection 4.5) reveals the complementary strengths of each component: OR excels at handling long deterministic lead times and avoids overfitting to noise, while LLMs excel under stochastic lead times and at detecting sudden demand shifts and leveraging world knowledge. Preregistered classroom experiments yield clean, statistically significant separations between collaboration modes (Subsection 5.2), demonstrating that both the LLM and the human add value to the decision pipeline.

Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes. We further investigate the role of humans through a controlled classroom experiment that embeds LLM recommendations into a human-in-the-loop decision pipeline. Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone. Beyond this population-level finding, we formalize an individual-level complementarity effect and derive a distribution-free lower bound on the fraction of individuals who benefit from AI collaboration; empirically, we find this fraction to be substantial. Taken together, our results provide evidence that effective inventory management benefits from a complementary system in which OR provides heuristics and calculators, LLMs contribute world knowledge and contextual reasoning, and human judgment adds value beyond automated decision making.

OR Algorithm LLM Agent Human Structural discipline: base-stock heuristics, mathematical precision Contextual reasoning: pattern detection, world knowledge Validation & oversight: catches LLM failures, detects anomalies recommendation reasoning / guidance Figure 2: The complementary interaction pattern of OR algorithms, LLM agents, and humans. On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. The finding that humans add value is particularly noteworthy: achieving genuine human–AI complementarity is widely recognized as difficult, with systematic evidence showing that human–AI teams frequently fail to outperform the better of human and AI acting independently (Bansal et al., 2021; Vaccaro et al., 2024; Hemmer et al., 2025). Our results suggest that inventory control—with its sequential decisions, delayed feedback, and rich contextual signals—is a setting where human judgment genuinely complements AI. First, InventoryBench2—our benchmark of 1,320 inventory instances, together with a public leaderboard to track progress—is available for the community to test and compare frontier LLMs, fine-tuning 2InventoryBench is available at https://tianyipeng.github.io/InventoryBench/.

Second, we open-source the web-based AI-Human Inventory Game3 used in our experiment (Figure 6), which can serve as a basis to be further developed for teaching and research in supply chain management. We design LLM agents for inventory control (Subsection 3.1), construct a benchmark of 1,320 instances to evaluate them (Subsection 4.1), and build a web-based game for human–AI interaction (Subsection 5.1). The OR→LLM pipeline achieves the best overall performance (Subsection 4.2), but disaggregating by lead time (Subsection 4.3), instance family (Subsection 4.4), and critical fractile (Subsection 4.5) reveals the complementary strengths of each component: OR excels at handling long deterministic lead times and avoids overfitting to noise, while LLMs excel under stochastic lead times and at detecting sudden demand shifts and leveraging world knowledge.

11. Controlled Citation Audit Cases

This section is a controlled capability demonstration, separate from the selected hypothesis. The system constructs one direct-support case, one uncertain or inferred case, and one deliberately wrong or nonexistent-reference case. These probes show whether the audit machinery can produce GREEN, YELLOW, and RED under known conditions. They should not be interpreted as extra scientific claims made by the hypothesis generator.

GREEN controlled caseKnown direct support case; should have evidence and pass as GREEN.
YELLOW controlled caseGenerated or inferred claim; should not be forced to GREEN.
RED controlled caseFake reference, contradiction, or unsupported absolute claim; should never become GREEN.
Failing controlled caseIndicates an audit rule regression rather than a scientific finding.

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Memory influenced query planning/context through same-scope paper hits or memory-derived queries.

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. They are warning-only memories: useful for avoiding repeated retrieval mistakes, identifying noisy sources, or explaining why a candidate was ignored, but never evidence that a scientific claim is false. Scientific support and contradiction must still come from current, inspectable evidence snippets.

16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • Evidence depth is limited: most evidence snippets come from abstracts rather than full text.
  • Reference extraction produced many unresolved fragments; bibliographic audit may be incomplete.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

This appendix expands evidence provenance and reference parsing details. It shows how the input seed was identified, how raw references were cleaned, every evidence record with source and location, resolved input references, verification snippets, and rejected evidence. Use it when checking whether an evidence number in the main report really corresponds to readable text from a traceable source.

Cleaned referenceReference parser produced a usable bibliographic string.
Unresolved fragmentLikely header, page number, broken DOI suffix, or incomplete citation; should not be treated as verified.
Rejected evidenceSnippet was boilerplate, front matter, off-topic, or low-value text and was excluded from support.

An order placed in Period N arrives during Period (N+L)’s arrival resolution, becomes visible in the ‘‘Period (N+L) conclude’’ message, and is read at the start of Period (N+L+1)’s decision phase. There is always a 1-period observation delay. Actual lead time may differ from promised; orders may also be lost (never arrive). 9(E-38926e21dd81)full_text_pdfinput_pdfAI Agents for Inventory Control:pdf:experiments:p40.95OR Algorithm LLM Agent Human Structural discipline: base-stock heuristics, mathematical precision Contextual reasoning: pattern detection, world knowledge Validation & oversight: catches LLM failures, detects anomalies recommendation reasoning / guidance Figure 2: The complementary interaction pattern of OR algorithms, LLM agents, and humans. On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. The finding that humans add value is particularly noteworthy: achieving genuine human–AI complementarity is widely recognized as difficult, with systematic evidence showing that human–AI teams frequently fail to outperform the better of human and AI acting independently (Bansal et al., 2021; Vaccaro et al., 2024; Hemmer et al., 2025). Our results suggest that inventory control—with its sequential decisions, delayed feedback, and rich contextual signals—is a setting where human judgment genuinely complements AI. First, InventoryBench2—our benchmark of 1,320 inventory instances, together with a public leaderboard to track progress—is available for the community to test and compare frontier LLMs, fine-tuning 2InventoryBench is available at https://tianyipeng.github.io/InventoryBench/.
10(E-36803b31616b)full_text_pdfinput_pdfAI Agents for Inventory Control:pdf:method:p10.95We study how OR algorithms, LLMs, and humans can interact and complement each other in a multi-period inventory control setting. We construct InventoryBench, a benchmark of over 1,000 inventory instances spanning both synthetic and real-world demand data, designed to stress-test decision rules under demand shifts, seasonality, and uncertain lead times. Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes. We further investigate the role of humans through a controlled classroom experiment that embeds LLM recommendations into a human-in-the-loop decision pipeline. Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone.
11(E-e361621c8d4d)full_text_pdfinput_pdfAI Agents for Inventory Control:pdf:experiments:p50.95We design LLM agents for inventory control (Subsection 3.1), construct a benchmark of 1,320 instances to evaluate them (Subsection 4.1), and build a web-based game for human–AI interaction (Subsection 5.1). The OR→LLM pipeline achieves the best overall performance (Subsection 4.2), but disaggregating by lead time (Subsection 4.3), instance family (Subsection 4.4), and critical fractile (Subsection 4.5) reveals the complementary strengths of each component: OR excels at handling long deterministic lead times and avoids overfitting to noise, while LLMs excel under stochastic lead times and at detecting sudden demand shifts and leveraging world knowledge. Preregistered classroom experiments yield clean, statistically significant separations between collaboration modes (Subsection 5.2), demonstrating that both the LLM and the human add value to the decision pipeline.

A OR Baseline: Data-driven Capped Base-stock Policy We consider a data-driven capped base-stock policy (Xin, 2021) as a default OR baseline to use for this inventory problem. Indeed, because we have lead times and lost sales, exact optimization is intractable. The capped base-stock policy is a simple heuristic that orders up to a “target” inventory position (accounting for in-transit orders) while also implementing a cap to prevent the algorithm from ordering too much at any one time, which would cause unstable inventory flow. At each period t, the data-driven version computes an order quantity as follows. Let ˆµt denote the estimated mean demand over the lead time horizon (specifically, the expected total demand from period t through period t + L), and let ˆσt denote the corresponding estimated standard deviation, recalling that L is the anticipated lead time. These estimates are computed from the historical demand observations {d−4, . . . , d0, d1, . . . , dt−1} as follows: • Let ¯d be the empirical mean of all observed demands. • Let sd be the empirical standard deviation of all observed demands. • Set ˆµt = (1 + L) ¯d and ˆσt = √ 1 + L sd, where the scaling factors (1 + L) and √ 1 + L arise from summing independent demands over 1 + L periods (Snyder and Shen, 2019). Define the critical fractile ρ = p/(p + h), which represents the optimal service level in the classical newsvendor model, and let z∗= Φ−1(ρ) where Φ is the cumulative distribution function of a standard normal random variable. The base-stock level is then Bt = ˆµt+z∗ˆσt, which is the inventory target (for on-hand plus in-transit inventory) that balances overstocking and understocking costs. To build intuition for readers unfamiliar with inventory theory: the critical fractile ρ determines the optimal frequency of overstocking versus stocking out. Under an optimal policy, the decision maker carries leftover inventory on a ρ fraction of periods and experiences a stockout on the remaining 1 −ρ fraction. When ρ is high (e.g., 0.95), stockouts are very costly relative to holding (because p is high relative to h), so the optimal policy maintains a large safety buffer and stocks out rarely. This is generally the case, which is why Newsvendor literature tends to place asymmetric focus on high values of ρ. When demand over the lead time horizon follows a normal distribution N(ˆµt, ˆσ2 t ) and lead time is zero, the base-stock level Bt = ˆµt + Φ−1(ρ) ˆσt is exactly optimalnot_found0.164Best bibliographic score 0.16; status not_found.
Let IPt denote the inventory position at time t, defined as on-hand inventory plus all outstanding orders: IPt = It + X τnot_found0.47Best bibliographic score 0.47; status not_found.
Justify final quantity by tying it to demand outlook, lead-time belief, and OR’s baseline. Carry-over insights: Record only NEW, evidence-backed insights about sustained shifts (demand mean/variance, lead time, seasonality). Stay conservative; provide concrete stats. Remove insights once they stop being true. Output format (JSON): "rationale": full step-by-step analysis "short rationale for human": 1--3 sentence summary "carry over insight": new sustained discoveries, or ‘‘’’ "action": {"{item id}": quantity} Human-in-the-loop additions. For Mode B (OR→LLM→Human), the system prompt appends instructions for a two-stage interaction: in Stage 1, the agent provides its initial rationale and decision; in Stage 2, if the human provides feedback, the agent incorporates it and outputs a revised action. For Mode C (autonomous with strategic guidance), the prompt instructs the agent to follow any strategic guidance from the human supervisor that appears at the top of the observation. Per-period user message. Each period, the user message provided to the LLM contains three blocks: (1) carry-over insights from prior periods (if any), formatted as a header listing each insight with its originating period; (2) the current observation from the game environment (on-hand inventory, in-transit orders, the previous period’s conclusion message reporting arrivals, demand, sales, and ending inventory); and (3) the OR algorithm’s recommendation with statistics (basestock level, current inventory position, empirical demand mean and standard deviation, and order cap). C LLM →OR Parameter Interface In the LLM →OR method, the LLM agent analyzes contextual information, demand history, and observed arrival patterns to provide parameter estimates that inform the OR algorithm’s calculations. The OR algorithm then uses these LLM-provided estimates to compute the order quantity according to the capped base-stock policy described in Section 3. The LLM can provide estimates for the following parameters: Lead time (L). The LLM provides an estimate of the effective lead time for the current order. This estimate may differ from the anticipated lead time if the LLM detects supply disruptions, seasonal delays, or patterns in past delivery performance. For example, if recent orders have consistently arrived late or been lost, the LLM may recommend using a longer effective lead time or adjusting the demand forecast accordingly. Mean demand over lead time horizon (ˆµt). The LLM provides a forecast of the expected total demand from period t through the period when the current order is expected to arrive (typically period t + L). This forecast may incorporate: • Pattern detection in recent demand (trends, changepoints, seasonality) • Product-specific knowledge (e.g., swimwear demand peaks in spring/summer) • Calendar effects (holidays, promotional periods) • Context from the text description xt The LLM’s forecast replaces or augments the simple empirical mean used by the default OR algorithm, allowing for adaptive forecasting that responds to non-stationary demand patterns. Standard deviation over lead time horizon (ˆσt). The LLM provides an estimate of demand variability over the lead time horizon. This estimate may reflect recent changes in demand volatility or structural shifts in the demand process. For instance, if demand has become more erratic in recent periods, the LLM may recommend a higher ˆσt to increase safety stock. Implementation note. The LLM may choose to specify only L, in which case ˆµt and ˆσt are computed using the OR baseline’s default empirical estimates: ˆµt = (1 + L) ¯d and ˆσt = √ 1 + L sd, where ¯d and sd are the sample mean and standard deviation of observed demand. Alternatively, if the LLM provides ˆµt and ˆσt directly, then the lead time estimate L serves only as contextual information and is not used in the base-stock calculation. This allows the LLM to bypass the assumption of stationary, IID demand implicit in the default formulas. Once the LLM provides these parameter estimates, the OR algorithm computes the base-stock level Bt = ˆµt+z∗ˆσt (where z∗= Φ−1(ρ)), determines inventory position IPt, and applies the capped base-stock policy to determine the order quantity qt. D Synthetic Instance Specifications This appendix provides the complete distributional specifications for all 40 synthetic demand variants used in the benchmark. We generate 10 demand pattern families, each with 4 parametric variants, yielding 40 unique distributions. For each, we create 2 independent demand realizations, cross them with 3 cost ratios (p:h = 1:1, 4:1, 19:1), and evaluate under 3 lead time settings (L = 0, L = 4, stochastic), producing 40 × 2 × 3 × 3 = 720 synthetic instances in total. Each instance consists of a T = 50 period test trajectory and 5 historical training samples. The 10 demand pattern families are: (1) stationary IID, (2–3) abrupt mean shifts (increase/decrease at period 16), (4–5) gradual trends (increasing/decreasing), (6) variance changes, (7) seasonal/cyclical patterns, (8) multiple changepoints, (9) temporary spikes or dips, and (10) autocorrelated AR(1) processes. Several of these patterns feature ad hoc demand shifts of the kind commonly encountered in operations management games—for example, the step-function demand increase in the Beer Game (Sterman, 1989) or the non-stationary demand environment in the Mexico-China sourcingnot_found0.162Best bibliographic score 0.16; status not_found.
game (Allon and Van Mieghem, 2010)—while others feature more predictable seasonality or gradual trends. The OR baseline described in Appendix A treats demand as stationary and IID, so it cannot detect any of these patterns. One could, in principle, augment the OR method with statistical changepoint detection, trend estimation, or models for correlated processes, but each extension requires its own methodology and careful tuning—there is no single off-the-shelf OR procedure that handles all 10 pattern families. By contrast, an LLM can attempt pattern recognition out of the box, without case-specific statistical machinery. Example 3 illustrates this capability with an example in which the LLM detects a mean shift, confirms the regime change over multiple periods, and adapts its forecast accordingly. Generation Procedure Demand realizations are generated as follows. For normal distributions, Dt ∼N(µ(t), σ(t)), truncated at zero and rounded to the nearest integer. For uniform distributions, Dt ∼Uniform[a, b], similarly truncated and rounded. For AR(1) processes, Dt = φ · Dt−1 + c + εt where εt ∼N(0, σ) and D0 = 100. Changepoint patterns apply the appropriate segment’s distribution based on period t. Training data generation depends on the pattern type. For stationary patterns (p01), training consists of 5 i.i.d. samples from the distribution. For changepoint patterns (p02, p03, p06, p08, p09), training consists of 5 i.i.d. samples from the first segment only—so the algorithm has no advance notice of the regime change. For trend (p04, p05), seasonal (p07), and AR(1) patterns (p10), training consists of sequential samples at t = 1, 2, 3, 4, 5. All random number generation uses base seed 42 for reproducibility. Pattern Specifications Pattern 1: Stationary IID (p01). Constant distribution throughout all periods. Fashion Recommendations dataset (Ling et al., 2022). We selected 200 distinct articles (SKUs), each with weekly aggregated sales data spanning 52 weeks: the first 5 weeks serve as historical training data and the remaining 47 weeks form the test trajectory (T = 47). Each article is evaluated under the same 3 lead time settings (0, 4, and stochastic), and cost ratios are uniformly randomly assigned (with fixed seed) corresponding to ρ ∈{0.50, 0.80, 0.95} (p:h = 1:1, 4:1, and 19:1), distributed evenly, producing 200 × 3 = 600 instances. Data Preprocessing We preprocessed the raw H&M transaction data as follows. We aggregated individual transactions into weekly sales counts per article for all of 2019, then applied two filters: (i) articles must have positive sales in at least 50 of 52 weeks (eliminating items with frequent stockouts or discontinuations), and (ii) articles must have stable prices, defined as a max-to-min weekly average price ratio of at most 1.2 after excluding up to 4 outlier holiday weeks (eliminating items whose demand is confounded by heavy discounting or price changes). We assume that observed sales is the true demand. From the articles passing both filters, we selected the top 200 by total annual sales volume. For each article, the first 5 weeks (2019-01-07 through 2019-02-04) form the training set and the remaining weeks (2019-02-11 through 2019-12-30) form the test trajectory. Each test instance also includes a text product description drawn from the H&M article metadata (product name, type, color, garment group, and a natural-language detail description), which the LLM can read but the OR algorithm ignores. The contextual information for each week also includes the actual real dates. LLM World Knowledge A key advantage of LLM-based methods on real instances is their ability to leverage world knowledge about product seasonality. Example 4 provides an illustrative example in which the LLM reasons about swimwear demand using calendar dates and seasonal knowledge to adjust its forecasts throughout the yearnot_found0.264Best bibliographic score 0.26; status not_found.

CL3 - YELLOW

We study how OR algorithms, LLMs, and humans can interact and complement each other in a multi-period inventory control setting. We construct InventoryBench, a benchmark of over 1,000 inventory instances spanning both synthetic and real-world demand data, designed to stress-test decision rules under demand shifts, seasonality, and uncertain lead times. Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes.
Evidence ID: 58 (E-3e5779c0f589); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:method:p1; Paper: AI Agents for Inventory Control:; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\AI Agents for Inventory Control(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Through this benchmark, we find that OR-augmented LLM methods outperform either method in isolation, suggesting that these methods are complementary rather than substitutes. We further investigate the role of humans through a controlled classroom experiment that embeds LLM recommendations into a human-in-the-loop decision pipeline. Contrary to prior findings that human-AI collaboration can degrade performance, we show that, on average, human-AI teams achieve higher profits than either humans or AI agents operating alone. Beyond this population-level finding, we formalize an individual-level complementarity effect and derive a distribution-free lower bound on the fraction of individuals who benefit from AI collaboration; empirically, we find this fraction to be substantial. Taken together, our results provide evidence that effective inventory management benefits from a complementary system in which OR provides heuristics and calculators, LLMs contribute world knowledge and contextual reasoning, and human judgment adds value beyond automated decision making.
Evidence ID: 59 (E-539c44be07b5); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:method:p1; Paper: AI Agents for Inventory Control:; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\AI Agents for Inventory Control(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Inventory control is a fundamental operations problem in which ordering decisions are traditionally guided by theoretically grounded operations research (OR) algorithms. However, such algorithms often rely on rigid modeling assumptions and can perform poorly when demand distributions shift or relevant contextual information is unavailable.
Evidence ID: 60 (E-1285a5960cd5); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: AI Agents for Inventory Control:; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\AI Agents for Inventory Control(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.95
We design LLM agents for inventory control (Subsection 3.1), construct a benchmark of 1,320 instances to evaluate them (Subsection 4.1), and build a web-based game for human–AI interaction (Subsection 5.1). The OR→LLM pipeline achieves the best overall performance (Subsection 4.2), but disaggregating by lead time (Subsection 4.3), instance family (Subsection 4.4), and critical fractile (Subsection 4.5) reveals the complementary strengths of each component: OR excels at handling long deterministic lead times and avoids overfitting to noise, while LLMs excel under stochastic lead times and at detecting sudden demand shifts and leveraging world knowledge. Preregistered classroom experiments yield clean, statistically significant separations between collaboration modes (Subsection 5.2), demonstrating that both the LLM and the human add value to the decision pipeline.
Evidence ID: 61 (E-3993870b3f96); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:experiments:p5; Paper: AI Agents for Inventory Control:; URL: D:\WeChat Files\wxid_6nut6ell2yad22\FileStorage\File\2026-06\AI Agents for Inventory Control(1).pdf; Confidence: 0.934
OR Algorithm LLM Agent Human Structural discipline: base-stock heuristics, mathematical precision Contextual reasoning: pattern detection, world knowledge Validation & oversight: catches LLM failures, detects anomalies recommendation reasoning / guidance Figure 2: The complementary interaction pattern of OR algorithms, LLM agents, and humans. On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. The finding that humans add value is particularly noteworthy: achieving genuine human–AI complementarity is widely recognized as difficult, with systematic evidence showing that human–AI teams frequently fail to outperform the better of human and AI acting independently (Bansal et al., 2021; Vaccaro et al., 2024; Hemmer et al., 2025). Our results suggest that inventory control—with its sequential decisions, delayed feedback, and rich contextual signals—is a setting where human judgment genuinely complements AI. On one hand, LLMs add substantial value to inventory control: they detect demand regime changes, incorporate world knowledge, and identify supply disruptions—capabilities that are generally difficult for traditional OR algorithms. The finding that humans add value is particularly noteworthy: achieving genuine human–AI complementarity is widely recognized as difficult, with systematic evidence showing that human–AI teams frequently fail to outperform the better of human and AI acting independently (Bansal et al., 2021; Vaccaro et al., 2024; Hemmer et al., 2025).
